border=\"0\"></img></a>\n</div><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/clusterstock/~4/MzTE9bXuItY\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 26 Aug 2020 06:42:44 +0000","feedId":13983,"bgimg":"https://i.insider.com/5f04f09cf0f419627b46a878?format=jpeg","linkMd5":"07184c94a533cb9e24bfa09b4da66b1a","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn94@2020_2/2020/08/26/09-00-39-836_7a0bc40c947e7bd1.webp","destWidth":2136,"destHeight":1602,"sourceBytes":153702,"destBytes":153702,"author":"insider@insider.com (Isobel Asher Hamilton)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/5f04f09cf0f419627b46a878?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn94@2020_2/2020/08/26/09-00-39-836_7a0bc40c947e7bd1.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn33@2020_6/2020/08/26/09-00-52-638_00f09200df8426be.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?i=MzTE9bXuItY:eOSz9bOFTHM:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn34@2020_4/2020/08/26/09-00-56-619_f75c50c0a4435398.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?i=MzTE9bXuItY:eOSz9bOFTHM:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn34@2020_5/2020/08/26/09-00-54-853_0dc096f950cc9c54.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?i=MzTE9bXuItY:eOSz9bOFTHM:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn85@2020_1/2020/08/26/09-00-48-792_cfa651eb443a049e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?d=cGdyc7Q-1BI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn13@2020_5/2020/08/26/09-00-52-311_767e235117808cfa.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?d=QXVau8BzmBE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn90@2020_6/2020/08/26/09-00-51-477_edf0fa9b5dde1c97.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/clusterstock/~4/MzTE9bXuItY":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn74@2020_1/2020/08/26/09-00-51-223_fee11ba115b27631.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598432350846},{"createdTime":"2020-08-26 16:59:10","updatedTime":"2020-08-26 16:59:10","title":"Etsy's CEO slams Amazon for trying to twist a California consumer protection bill into a tool to 'wipe out its competitors'","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/etsy-ceo-amazon-using-california-bill-to-wipe-out-competitors-2020-8","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5ee0f6a62618b95f1c362813?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Jeff Bezos\" height=\"1980\" width=\"3000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Lawmakers are looking to hold Amazon accountable for defective products sold on its online marketplace.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Mandel Ngan/AFP via Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\"><li>Etsy CEO Josh Silverman accused Amazon of attempting to stifle competition by lobbying California lawmakers to make a proposed consumer protection bill overly burdensome for smaller players.</li><li>In a <a href=\"https://medium.com/etsy-impact/a-wolf-in-sheeps-clothing-california-s-ab-3262-consumer-protection-bill-will-empower-amazon-to-c131ffedc3dd\">blog post</a> Tuesday, Silverman said Amazon was \"taking bold steps to wipe out its competitors by promoting complex, hard-to-comply-with legislation that only they can afford to absorb.\"</li><li>The bill, <a href=\"http://leginfo.legislature.ca.gov/faces/billNavClient.xhtml?bill_id=201920200AB3262\">AB-3262</a>, would hold online marketplaces liable for defective products similarly to physical retailers, but Silverman said the version Amazon lobbied for will ultimately hurt small businesses.</li><li>A California court ruled earlier this month that <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/court-rules-amazon-liable-defective-third-party-seller-product-sales-2020-8\">Amazon is liable for defective products</a> sold by third-parties on its site due to the \"pivotal\" role it played in transactions.</li><li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li></ul><p>Amazon came under fire Tuesday from&#160;Etsy CEO Josh Silverman, who alleged in a <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/court-rules-amazon-liable-defective-third-party-seller-product-sales-2020-8\">blog post</a> that the company was trying to turn a proposed California consumer protection bill into a \"wolf in sheep's clothing\" that would stifle competition.</p><p>\"Amazon is taking bold steps to wipe out its competitors by promoting complex, hard-to-comply-with legislation that only they can afford to absorb. Amazon's goal is to be the only place to buy stuff online,\" Silverman wrote.</p><p>Amazon did not respond to Business Insider's request for comment.</p><p>In California, lawmakers are expected to vote this week on <a href=\"http://leginfo.legislature.ca.gov/faces/billNavClient.xhtml?bill_id=201920200AB3262\">AB-3262</a>, a bill that would make online marketplaces liable for defective products sold on their sites by third parties &#8212; like brick-and-mortar stores and most retailers are already &#8212; which would be the first such law in the US.</p><p>The original version of the bill exempted online sales of auctioned and handmade goods, as well as classified ads sites, meaning most products sold on marketplaces like Etsy, eBay, and Craigslist.</p><p>But on Monday, days after Amazon said in a <a href=\"https://blog.aboutamazon.com/policy/amazon-stands-ready-to-support-ab-3262-if-all-stores-are-held-to-the-same-standards\">blog post</a> that it would support a version of the bill that applied to all online marketplaces, lawmakers amended AB-3262 to do exactly that.</p><p>Silverman accused Amazon of \"working back channels to redirect this legislation to their advantage\" by lobbying lawmakers to extend its scope to all stores, which he said would harm smaller marketplaces who don't have the resources to comply with its complicated provisions as well as small businesses and consumers onto whom smaller marketplaces would likely pass off those new costs.</p><p>\"While AB 3262 will be an inconvenience to the e-commerce behemoth, it could be crushing to smaller e-commerce players,\" Silverman wrote.</p><p>\"This is an abuse of market power play,\" he added, noting recent criticism of Amazon's potentially anti-competitive behavior from <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/tech-antitrust-hearing-recap-bezos-cook-zuckerberg-pichai-testimony-2020-7#:~:text=Wednesday's%20tech%20antitrust%20hearing%20was,about%20their%20companies'%20market%20power.\">lawmakers</a>, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/amazon-sellers-slam-ceo-jeff-bezos-following-antitrust-testimony-2020-7\">third-party sellers</a>, and <a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/amazon-tech-startup-echo-bezos-alexa-investment-fund-11595520249\">startups</a> the company has invested in.</p><p>\"Injured consumers should be able to seek compensation regardless of how a particular online marketplace makes money,\" Amazon head of public policy Brian Huseman argued in the Friday blog post. \"For AB 3262 to be a successful, lasting, and meaningful law, it cannot leave open loopholes for some marketplaces to escape accountability.\"</p><p>Amazon has <a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-amazon-dodges-responsibility-for-unsafe-products-the-case-of-the-hoverboard-11575563270\">successfully evaded legal liability</a> for dangerous and defective products for years, but a California court ruled earlier this month that the retail giant should be on the hook after a <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/court-rules-amazon-liable-defective-third-party-seller-product-sales-2020-8\">woman alleged she was severely burned</a> when a replacement laptop battery she purchased on Amazon exploded.</p><p>Amazon argued in the case that it wasn't liable because \"it did not distribute, manufacture, or sell the product,\" which was sold by a third-party seller, but the court disagreed.</p><p>\"Whatever term we use to describe Amazon's role, be it 'retailer,' 'distributor,' or merely 'facilitator,' it was pivotal in bringing the product here to the consumer,\" the judge in the case concluded.</p><p>In recent years, more pressure has mounted on lawmakers and regulators to hold online marketplaces and tech platforms &#8212; such as <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/doj-section-230-protections-facebook-twitter-trump-report-2020-6-1029318136\">Facebook and Twitter</a>, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/uber-lyft-ab5-fight-reveals-dependence-full-time-drivers-2020-8\">Uber and Lyft</a>, and <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/airbnb-sues-guest-party-after-3-injured-in-shooting-2020-8\">Airbnb</a> &#8212; accountable for how third parties behave while using their services.</p><p>Like Etsy, smaller competitors have pushed back on similar regulatory efforts by arguing that they would ultimately be hurt while the intended targets &#8212; large incumbents with massive legal teams and deep pockets &#8212; would be more able to adapt or absorb legal penalties for violating the laws.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a 04:35:22 +0000","feedId":13983,"bgimg":"https://i.insider.com/5f166040988ee32151123983?format=jpeg","linkMd5":"68a8d18cddd4b69bfcbfcf54bbc5cf94","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn54@2020_1/2020/08/26/09-00-43-413_f6af20fa17e052f1.webp","destWidth":1993,"destHeight":1495,"sourceBytes":72630,"destBytes":72630,"author":"tsonnemaker@businessinsider.com (Tyler Sonnemaker)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/5ee0f6a62618b95f1c362813?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn1@2020_4/2020/08/26/09-00-56-134_eaa8ffffc675ee04.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn33@2020_6/2020/08/26/09-00-52-638_00f09200df8426be.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?i=RO01Cy9b3J4:8mRZxRhMT3M:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn49@2020_6/2020/08/26/09-00-53-060_a69afbe3d095e97a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?i=RO01Cy9b3J4:8mRZxRhMT3M:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn62@2020_4/2020/08/26/09-00-50-680_eafb480bd60e79be.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?i=RO01Cy9b3J4:8mRZxRhMT3M:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn66@2020_2/2020/08/26/09-00-53-377_87a0dd9e0ac61eb4.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?d=cGdyc7Q-1BI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn13@2020_5/2020/08/26/09-00-52-311_767e235117808cfa.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/clusterstock?d=QXVau8BzmBE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn90@2020_6/2020/08/26/09-00-51-477_edf0fa9b5dde1c97.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/clusterstock/~4/RO01Cy9b3J4":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn29@2020_2/2020/08/26/09-00-56-732_e762c153f859c87e.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598432350848},{"createdTime":"2020-08-26 16:59:10","updatedTime":"2020-08-26 16:59:10","title":"The 6-foot social distancing rule is based on nearly 80-year-old science. Scientists at MIT and Oxford have created a traffic light system to use instead.","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/6-foot-distancing-rule-is-outdated-oxford-mit-new-system-2020-8","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f45772b42f43f001ddfeff5?format=jpeg\" alt=\"social distancing\" height=\"1467\" width=\"2200\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Academics study in the socially distanced reading rooms at the Bodleian Libraries on August 25, 2020 in Oxford, England. The world famous libraries closed in mid-March due to the coronavirus pandemic. After putting in strict social distancing measures the Oxford University Libraries have partially opened for students and academics.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Christopher Furlong/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\"><li>The 6-foot rule for social distancing is based on science that's 80 years old.&#160;</li><li>Researchers say it's an antiquated way to think about how viruses move through the air.</li><li>Instead, we should be considering many facets of an interaction: where it takes place, how long it lasts, and whether it's going to be loud.&#160;</li><li>A new tool out from researchers at Oxford and MIT provides a traffic light system for assessing risks during the pandemic, so we don't always have to be on high alert.&#160;</li><li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li></ul><p>In the late 1800s, German scientist <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2843953/#RSIF20090407C19\">Carl Fl&#252;gge had a hunch</a>: maybe if you maintain enough physical distance between people who are sick and those who are well, you can prevent the spread of pathogens from person to person.</p><p>At the time, it was just a hypothesis, one that scientists like him often tried to test out using glass plates.</p><p>But it would take another four decades for technology to advance enough to confirm the idea, with the advent of high-speed photography.&#160;</p><p>In the early 1940s, scientists finally got their first glimpses of people's sneezes hurtling through the air in real time, at a capture rate of 30,000 frames per second, confirming that indeed, most of the stuff that we throw into the air when we sneeze, cough, or yell, tends to settle down to the ground within about a wingspan or so (say, 3 to 6 feet).</p><p><a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1531372/\">Here</a> is one of those (revolting) first images:&#160;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f457b5842f43f001ddff01b?format=jpeg\" alt=\"one of the first sneeze pictures\" height=\"1650\" width=\"2200\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>This photo of a sneezer caught in the act was taken by Professor Marshall Jennison from MIT, and published in a 1941 research paper.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Bettmann/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><h2>When 'the dogma was born'</h2><p>Back then, scientists maintained that most of the infectious gunk people expel (say, around 90% of their pathogens) travel less than six feet away.</p><p>Their study measurements were never meant to be taken as hard and fast rules about how far we should stand from other people during a pandemic, though.&#160;</p><p>Nevertheless, these three-to-six-foot rules of thumb have become easy-to-follow protocols for keeping potentially sick people at arm's length during the coronavirus outbreak.&#160;</p><p>\"The dogma was born,\" Professor Lidia Morawaska, a <a href=\"https://academic.oup.com/cid/advance-article/doi/10.1093/cid/ciaa939/5867798\">leading aerosol scientist</a> in Australia, said of the 80-year-old 6-foot rule. \"Like any dogma, it's extremely difficult to change people's minds, and change the dogmas.\"&#160;</p><p>But as the coronavirus pandemic drags on for months on end, Morawaska and other leading air and virus scientists and engineers are starting to lead a charge towards dismantling the old six-foot, dogmatic rule, and taking a more thoughtful, nuanced approach to managing the novel coronavirus's spread.</p><p>Instead of always being on super-high alert, or assuming that a distance of six feet (or wearing masks, or washing hands) keeps us 100% safe all the time, we should be learning how better to assess the situations we're in every day, letting our guard down now and again when it's relatively safe, and moving back onto high-alert when it's appropriate.&#160;</p><h2>Can we start thinking differently?</h2><p>On Tuesday, hoping to inject a little more of such empowerment into the ways that people protect themselves from the virus' spread, <a href=\"https://www.eurekalert.org/pub_releases/2020-08/b-rsd082420.php\">researchers from Oxford and MIT released a new traffic light system</a> that they hope will help people do away with antiquated dogmas, and live life to its fullest, while still being careful <em>enough</em> during the pandemic.&#160;</p><p>\"With knowledge and tools that are relatively simple to use, that distill complex information, our hope is that decision makers, local community leaders, school leaders, and everybody that is organizing anything like a barbecue or party or a wedding, is empowered to be more resilient, by having the tools to make the right decisions and to impose the right restrictions,\" Professor Lydia Bourouiba, Director of the <a href=\"https://lbourouiba.mit.edu/home\">Fluid Dynamics of Disease Transmission&#160;Lab</a> at MIT, told Business Insider, shortly before her <a href=\"https://www.bmj.com/content/370/bmj.m3223\">new tool was released in the BMJ</a>.&#160;</p><p>\"We equip people with understanding to adapt in various situations so that they know when they need to be absolutely vigilant, and when they can let their guard down,\" she said.&#160;</p><p>The most important things to keep in mind when assessing the riskiness of any situation include taking a look at the environment you're in, acknowledging the density of the crowd, and the activities that people are doing.&#160;</p><h2>The coronavirus doesn't follow binary hard-and-fast rules about infection, and neither should you</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5ebefc9e988ee3172d5a3ee9?format=jpeg\" alt=\"pool noodles germany\" height=\"541\" width=\"721\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/Cafe-Konditorei-Rothe-302537903117077/\" target=\"_blank\">Café &#38; Konditorei Rothe</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Environmental scientists like Morawaska say when it comes to talking about how the coronavirus spreads, there's no point in trying to distinguish <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/who-coronavirus-may-be-airborne-could-float-indoors-2020-7\">a droplet from an aerosol</a>, or distinguish what happens at three feet away versus 10. What matters most is how much virus has a chance to get into your body, regardless of how it gets there.</p><p>\"There are three modes of transmission, and all three modes of transmission have to be controlled,\" she said.</p><p>Those three modes include people (the <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/how-coronavirus-spreads-droplets-aerosols-poop-2020-5\">most common source of infection</a>), surfaces, and the air.&#160;</p><p>\"These things happen at the same time, and therefore distinguishing what's what is very difficult,\" Morawaska said.</p><p>Life is more dangerous, then, in places where people become animated, excited, or otherwise loud in close confines, with stale air.&#160;</p><p>\"Breathing out, singing, coughing, and sneezing generate warm, moist, high momentum gas clouds of exhaled air containing respiratory droplets,\" Bourouiba and her co-authors wrote in the BMJ.&#160;</p><p>In such instances, a distance of even 20 to 30 feet may not be enough to protect you from an infection.&#160;</p><p>Meatpacking plants are then understandably ripe for viral spread, because \"the combination of high levels of worker contagion, poor ventilation, cramped working conditions, background noise (which leads to shouting), and low compliance with mask wearing\" all contribute to viral spread, Bourouiba and her co-authors noted.</p><p>The same issue pops up easily in bars, gyms, indoor music venues, churches, and clubs.&#160;</p><h2>We need to be able to adapt to different environments with different levels of vigilance, so we're not constantly on max alert</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f1afd85f0f4190ad95e38b3?format=jpeg\" alt=\"coronavirus risk infographic\" height=\"1134\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A different risk chart, similar to the one that Bourouiba and her co-authors invented for the BMJ, which weighs the relative risks of different activities during the pandemic, based on various criteria.\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"https://www.covid19reopen.com/\" target=\"_blank\">Dr. Ezekiel Emanuel - University of Pennsylvania, Dr. James Phillips - George Washington University, Saskia Popescu - University of Arizona/George Mason University</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Engineers, virologists, and environmental scientists all stress that learning how to live with the virus can be done.</p><p>\"I think everybody understands what traffic lights are in the general public,\" Bourouiba said.&#160;</p><p>She says it's important to have such guides in mind as we weigh how to reopen, gather, and socialize during the pandemic, keeping both our environment, and our behaviors in it, top of mind.</p><p>\"We need to be able to adapt so that we are not constantly at max alert,\" she said. \"Not just the six foot rule, or the mask on its own.\" she added.&#160;</p><p>The simple questions to ask include: How long is the contact? Are people wearing face masks? Is the setting well-ventilated? And will it be quiet or loud?&#160;</p><p>\"It's not rocket science to work out what needs to be done to minimize the risk of infection transmission,\" Morawaska said.&#160;</p><div 16:59:10","updatedTime":"2020-08-26 16:59:10","title":"Trump exploited the powers of the presidency and the backdrop of the White House to promote his campaign during night 2 of the RNC","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/trump-abused-the-powers-of-the-presidency-a-the-republican-convention-2020-8","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f45c6ba42f43f001ddff0be?format=jpeg\" alt=\"U.S. President Donald Trump looks on as five candidates for naturalization coming from five different countries recite the oath of allegiance during the largely virtual 2020 Republican National Convention broadcast from Washington, U.S. August 25, 2020.\" height=\"640\" width=\"1280\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>President Donald Trump watching five candidates for naturalization from five different countries reciting the oath of allegiance during the 2020 Republican National Convention on Tuesday.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Republican National Convention/Reuters Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\"><li>President Donald Trump made two surprise appearances on the second night of the Republican National Convention &#8212; and in both he broke ethical norms by using the powers of the presidency to promote his reelection campaign.</li><li>In the first instance, Trump pardoned Jon Ponder, a bank robber turned activist, at the White House. The move provoked widespread criticism that Trump had abused his office for partisan purposes.</li><li>In perhaps the most blatant ethics violation of the night, Trump had his acting secretary of homeland security conduct a naturalization ceremony for five new US citizens at the White House.</li><li>A federal law known as the Hatch Act bars federal employees &#8212; excluding the president &#8212; from engaging in any political activities, including campaigning, in their official capacities.</li><li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li></ul><p>President Donald Trump made two surprise appearances during the second night of the Republican National Convention &#8212; and in both he broke ethical norms by using the powers of the presidency to promote his reelection campaign.</p><p>In the first instance, Trump pardoned Jon Ponder, a bank robber turned activist, at the White House. The move provoked widespread criticism that Trump was abusing his office for purely partisan purposes.</p><p>In perhaps the most <a href=\"https://twitter.com/mmurraypolitics/status/1298442255926206466\">blatant ethics violation</a> of the night, Trump had his acting secretary of homeland security, Chad Wolf, conduct a naturalization ceremony for five new US citizens at the White House. In the prerecorded video, Wolf announced the new American citizens in his official capacity and Trump delivered remarks congratulating them.</p><p>A federal law known as the <a href=\"https://osc.gov/Services/Pages/HatchAct.aspx\">Hatch Act</a> bars federal employees &#8212; excluding the president &#8212; from engaging in any political activities, including campaigning, in their official capacities.</p><p>Later on Tuesday night, Secretary of State Mike Pompeo <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/pompeo-rnc-speech-jerusalsem-israel-video-hatch-act-2020-8\">delivered a speech</a> celebrating Trump's presidency from a rooftop in Jerusalem, where he is on a four-day taxpayer-funded diplomatic trip to the Middle East. State Department aides said Pompeo was acting in his personal capacity.</p><p>It's unprecedented for sitting cabinet secretaries to appear at presidential nominating conventions or to use their offices for clearly partisan purposes.</p><p>Ethics experts have broadly condemned the president and his campaign's apparent disregard for ethical norms and laws.</p><p>\"A reminder that using government locations, government functions, and executive branch officials as part of a giant political campaign event is anti-democratic and in many cases illegal,\" <a href=\"https://twitter.com/NoahBookbinder/status/1298447703316344833\">tweeted</a> Noah Bookbinder, the executive director of Citizens for Responsibility and Ethics in Washington. \"They keep blowing through law and democratic principles and assume no one will care.\"</p><p>Walter Shaub, who served as director of the US Office of Government Ethics under President Barack Obama, tweeted on Tuesday night that he'd \"seen a lot of ethical abuses before\" but that he'd \"never seen anything like that.\"</p><p>\"Chad Wolf not only used his official authority for politics, he specifically hijacked a governmental function for the Trump campaign. And he used humans,\" Shaub said, adding that low-level federal employees were being held to much higher ethical standards than those in the White House.</p><p>But some of the president's top aides have openly dismissed the Hatch Act, The New York Times <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2020/08/22/us/politics/republican-convention-preview.html\">recently reported</a>. Trump's adviser Kellyanne Conway has also <a href=\"https://osc.gov/News/Pages/19-10-Kellyanne-Conway-Hatch-Act.aspx\">repeatedly violated</a> the ethics law.</p><p>On Tuesday, House Democrats <a href=\"https://twitter.com/JoshNBCNews/status/1298336391848484865\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"100\" data-uri=\"620b0fea63cd32f0b82c33d1b7f7b23d\">announced</a> they had opened an investigation into the legality of Pompeo's speech.</p><p>Ethics experts had already taken issue with the fact the president and first lady planned to deliver keynote addresses from the White House.</p><section data-track-content=\"\" data-post-type=\"post\"><p>Trump appeared in multiple videos shown Monday that were filmed inside the White House, and he's expected to deliver the convention's keynote address on Thursday from the South Lawn. Melania Trump was scheduled to speak later Tuesday night from the Rose Garden at the White House.</p></section><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a 16:59:10","updatedTime":"2020-08-26 16:59:10","title":"Melania Trump calls for four more years of Trump in a solemn Republican convention speech","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/melania-trump-republican-convention-rnc-speech-video-2020-8","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f45d2d889aff80028ab7f15?format=jpeg\" alt=\"U.S. first lady Melania Trump delivers her live address to the 2020 Republican National Convention from the White House in Washington\" height=\"640\" width=\"1280\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>U.S. first lady Melania Trump delivers her live address to the 2020 Republican National Convention from the White House in Washington\n<p class=\"copyright\">KEVIN LAMARQUE/Reuters Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\"><li>First Lady Melania Trump celebrated her husband's record as president and offered a solemn call to action at the Republican National Convention on Tuesday night.&#160;</li><li>The first lady offered sympathy to those impacted by the pandemic, asked Americans to \"focus on our future\" and move past \"racial unrest,\" and called her husband an \"honest\" and \"authentic\" leader.&#160;</li><li>\"Whether you like it or not, you always know what he's thinking,\" she said of the president, eliciting laughs.&#160;</li><li>Melania's calm, sober speech, which closed the night, contrasted with the first two days of a Republican convention dominated by dark, fear-inducing messaging about Democrats.&#160;</li><li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li></ul><p>First Lady Melania Trump celebrated her husband's record as president and offered a solemn call to action at the Republican National Convention on Tuesday night.&#160;</p><p>\"I'm here because we need my husband to be our president and commander for four more years,\" she said.&#160;</p><p>Unlike most speakers at the Republican convention, Trump acknowledged the severity of the Covid-19 crisis and offered sympathy for those who've been impacted by it. She specifically offered encouragement to mothers faced with schooling their children at home this fall.&#160;</p><p>Trump's speech, controversially delivered in the <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/video-melania-trumps-renovations-to-the-white-house-rose-garden-2020-8\">newly-renovated</a> Rose Garden at the White House, was heavy in praise for her husband and light on policy. About 50 supporters, including the president and other family members, Vice President Mike Pence, and Second Lady Karen Pence attended her speech in person.&#160;</p><p>Ethics experts <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/trump-abused-the-powers-of-the-presidency-a-the-republican-convention-2020-8\">have criticized the president</a> and first lady for using the White House as the backdrop for their RNC appearance this week &#8212; a possible violation of the Hatch Act.&#160;</p><p>The first lady mentioned her experiences visiting members of the military and their families \"around the world\" and those in hospitals, and thanked them for \"inspiring\" her. She told the story of a young hospitalized boy she met in Rome, Italy who received a heart transplant shortly after she visited him.&#160;</p><p>Trump focused on her signature \"Be Best\" campaign, which raises awareness about online bullying and opioid addiction, particularly among children. She said her initiative had shined a light on people and organizations who are working to protect children.&#160;</p><p>\"I continue to believe that by shining a light on these positive examples, others across the country and the globe will be inspired to do their part,\" she said. \"Helping children is not a political goal.\"&#160;</p><p>The initiative doesn't have any concrete policy aims and <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/trump-opposed-melania-cyberbullying-campaign-because-of-his-twitter-habits-2018-8\">critics have accused</a> the first lady of hypocrisy for discouraging cyber bullying while her husband uses his Twitter account to attack his critics and political opponents.&#160;</p><p>The first lady said in her second term in the White House, she'd like to work with states to pass legislation to protect children from addiction, abuse and neglect.&#160;</p><p>She also spoke about the \"racial unrest\" in the country and urged Americans to \"focus on our future, while still learning from our past.\"&#160;</p><p>She added, \"I also ask people to stop the looting and violence done in the name of injustice.\"&#160;</p><p>\"While the debates rage on about the issues of race, let's focus on the progress we have made,\" she said.&#160;</p><p>She concluded the address by praising her husband's \"honesty\" and \"authenticity.\"</p><p>\"Whether you like it or not, you always know what he's thinking,\" she said, an apparent joke about his tweets.&#160;</p><p>Trump's speech, which closed the night, contrasted with the first two days of the Republican convention, which were dominated by dark warnings of a socialist takeover, crime, and chaos if the president loses reelection.&#160;</p><p>The second night of the convention featured a series of speakers who highlighted partisan divisions on key issues including immigration and abortion, and attacked Democrats as socialists.&#160;&#160;</p><p>Tuesday night's address was the first lady's most prominent public remarks since her scandal-plagued 2016 convention speech, which included a passage that appeared to be plagiarized from former First Lady Michelle Obama's 2008 Democratic convention speech. (A Trump campaign official was blamed for the alleged plagiarism).&#160;</p><p>The first lady rarely speaks at length in public and has remained almost entirely out of the public eye in recent weeks. She's more private and less involved in White House affairs than any other first lady in modern history. The former model and jewelry designer hosts fewer events, travels less, almost never sits for an interview, and rarely campaigns for her husband.&#160;</p><p>She has publicly disagreed with her husband's policies on a few occasions, including publicly criticizing his administration's separation of migrant children from their parents and calling for peaceful demonstrations over police brutality as the president threatened violence against protesters.&#160;</p><p>Trump's speech comes as her former close friend and adviser, socialite Stephanie Winston Wolkoff, prepares to release a book documenting their tumultuous relationship, which reportedly ended after Wolkoff raked in millions for her work on the 2017 inauguration. Wolkoff said she was \"thrown under the bus\" by the first lady amid scrutiny of the inauguration's spending.&#160;</p><p>The book's publisher, Simon &#38; Schuster, says Wolkoff will provide new details about Melania's reactions to the Access Hollywood tape, her husband's alleged affairs and hush money payments, and why the first lady wore a controversial jacket with the words, \"I really don't care, do u?\" printed on it.&#160;</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a 16:59:10","updatedTime":"2020-08-26 16:59:10","title":"Google's own engineers admitted that the company 'confuses users' on privacy settings that are now the subject of a lawsuit","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/google-engineers-admit-privacy-settings-confuse-users-in-legal-docs-2020-8","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5ee28d134dca68439f463e33?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Sundar Pichai\" height=\"1916\" width=\"2555\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Google&#39;s data collection practices are coming under increasing scrutiny from lawmakers and regulators.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Yves Herman/Reuters</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\"><li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/arizona-sues-google-over-alleged-illegal-phone-location-data-tracking-2020-5\">Arizona sued Google</a> in May, accusing it of deceiving users about privacy settings and collecting their location data even after they opted out.</li><li>In a <a href=\"https://www.azag.gov/sites/default/files/2020-08/20-08-21_Complaint_Redacted.pdf\">less redacted version</a> of the lawsuit released last week and first reported by the <a href=\"https://www.azmirror.com/2020/08/24/unsealed-google-lawsuit-docs-show-its-own-engineers-were-confused-by-privacy-settings/\">Arizona Mirror</a>, Google's own engineers admitted that the company \"confuses users\" and even employees.</li><li>One engineer said that Google's user interface \"feels like it is designed to make things possible, yet difficult enough that people won't figure it out.\"</li><li>Google also scrambled to respond to a 2018 report by the <a href=\"https://apnews.com/828aefab64d4411bac257a07c1af0ecb/AP-Exclusive:-Google-tracks-your-movements,-like-it-or-not\">Associated Press</a> on the misleading privacy settings that sparked the lawsuit, calling an \"oh sh--\" meeting and tracking the story's reach on social media.</li><li>Google is currently embroiled in several lawsuits over how it uses data and has been hit with several multi-billion dollar fines in recent years.</li><li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li></ul><p>New information has surfaced in a lawsuit involving Google's data collection and location tracking practices, including admissions by the company's own engineers that its privacy settings were confusing to both users and employees.</p><p>In May, Arizona Attorney General Mark Brnovich's office sued Google, accusing the company of deceiving users about when and how it tracked their locations and continuing to track them even after they tried to opt out.</p><p>In a version of the lawsuit that was made public on Friday with additional sections unredacted, multiple Google employees acknowledged that Google's privacy settings and design choices made it difficult for users to actually opt out of location tracking.</p><p>\"The current [user interface] feels like it is designed to make things possible, yet difficult enough that people won't figure it out,\" one employee said, according to the new complaint, which was earlier reported by the <a href=\"https://www.azmirror.com/2020/08/24/unsealed-google-lawsuit-docs-show-its-own-engineers-were-confused-by-privacy-settings/\">Arizona Mirror</a>.</p><p>\"Definitely confusing from a user point of view if we need googlers [to] explain it to us,\" another said.</p><p>\"Privacy controls have long been built into our services and our teams work continuously to discuss and improve them,\" a Google spokesman told the Arizona Mirror. \"In the case of location information, we've heard feedback, and have worked hard to improve our privacy controls. In fact, even these cherry picked published extracts state clearly that the team's goal was to 'Reduce confusion around Location History Settings.'\"</p><p>The lawsuit stemmed in part from a 2018 report by the <a href=\"https://apnews.com/828aefab64d4411bac257a07c1af0ecb/AP-Exclusive:-Google-tracks-your-movements,-like-it-or-not\">Associated Press</a> that found Google continued to track users even after they explicitly changed a privacy setting telling it not to by collecting location information from other Google products.</p><p>While Google defended itself in a statement to the AP at the time, newly unredacted evidence in the lawsuit reveals that even some employees felt the company's settings were deceptive.</p><p>\"I agree with the article. Location off should mean location off, not except for this case or that case,\" one employee said.</p><p>\"Speaking as a user, WTF? More specifically I **thought** I had location tracking turned off on my phone,\" one&#160; engineer said, according to the Arizona Mirror, adding: \"So our messaging around this is enough to confuse a privacy focused (Google software engineer). That's not good.\"</p><p>The attorney general's office alleged in the complaint that \"even top-level Google employees do not understand under what conditions Google collects location data.\"</p><p><a href=\"https://assets.documentcloud.org/documents/20385392/arizona-v-google-exhibits-20-23.pdf\">New exhibits</a> released along with the less redacted complaint also showed how Google scrambled to respond to the AP article, holding an \"oh sh--\" meeting to discuss its PR reponse and changes to its privacy settings.</p><p>\"Both comms and policy are looking for an update on where we are in terms of fixing 'location history' fixes and having one single place to turn off instead of 3,\" one employee wrote in a recap of the meeting, according to the exhibit.</p><p>Google also tracked the story's reach in daily reports, according to <a href=\"https://cdn.arstechnica.net/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/arizona-v-google-exhibit-219-225.pdf\">another exhibit</a>, identifying more than 60,000 mentions on social media and characterizing 100% of related media coverage as \"negative.\"</p><p>This is not the first time Google has faced legal scrutiny over how it collects and uses data.</p><p>Last December, the European Union opened an <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/google-faces-eu-investigation-over-collection-of-data-2019-12\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"100\" data-uri=\"0d29e179cb4b558b169bb0fa2944436a\">antitrust investigation</a>&#160;into how Google leverages data months after slapping the company with its third multibillion-dollar fine in just three years.</p><p>Regulators in the US have also been looking into whether Google's data practices unfairly give it an edge in its online ads and search businesses. A group of <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/state-attorneys-general-launch-antitrust-probe-into-google-2019-9\">state attorneys general</a> (including Brnovich) and the US Justice Department both have <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/google-might-be-hit-with-two-antitrust-lawsuits-2020-5\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"100\" data-uri=\"47622f94f12abc63d60af0c7b2a6bef2\">ongoing investigations</a>&#160;that are expected to wrap up in the coming months and could result in additional lawsuits.</p><p>Sundar Pichai, the former CEO of Google who now leads its parent company, Alphabet, also faced a <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/google-ceo-sundar-pichai-tough-grilling-in-antitrust-opener-2020-7\">tough grilling from lawmakers</a> last month as part of a congressional&#160;hearing focused on potential anti-competitive behavior by Google, Amazon, Apple, and Facebook.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the
